Define the state after turn n as (S_n, Q_n). The players' constraints involve n. Maybe we can consider the following:

At odd n, Alice's move must keep S_n ≤ λ n. At even n, Bazza's move must keep Q_n ≤ n.

Consider the "potential" P_n = S_n - λ n (for odd n) and P_n = Q_n - n (for even n). Actually the constraints are that after the player's move, the appropriate quantity is ≤ bound. So the game ends when a player cannot move, i.e., when before his move, the opposite sum already exceeds the bound (since he must add nonnegative). For Alice, if before her odd move, S_{n-1} > λ n, she loses. For Bazza, if before his even move, Q_{n-1} > n, he loses. So we can define the "danger" state.

Maybe we can analyze the game as a "two-player game" where each player can control one of the sums, and the other sum is the "resource" that the opponent can use. This is reminiscent of a "game of balancing" where the outcome is determined by the ratio of λ to some constant.

Let's try to formalize.

Let’s denote the sequence x_i. The game proceeds as long as the inequalities hold. So if the game goes on forever, then for all n, S_n ≤ λ n if n odd, and Q_n ≤ n if n even. But also, note that the other inequality (the one not required by the player) might hold as a consequence? Not necessarily.

We can consider that each player's move influences the next bound. For instance, at turn 2, Bazza's choice of x2 determines S2 and Q2. Then at turn 3, Alice's bound is λ*3. She can choose x3 up to λ*3 - S2. She will try to avoid making Q3 exceed 4, etc.

We can view the game as a "turn-based" game where each player's objective is to make the opponent's sum exceed the bound. That is, for Bazza, he wants to make S_n > λ n for some odd n; for Alice, she wants to make Q_n > n for some even n.

We can try to find a "strategy" for one player that guarantees a win, given λ.

In this strategy, Alice always plays max on her turns, and Bazza always plays max on his turns. The recurrence:

Let S_n, Q_n be after n turns. At odd n, Alice chooses x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. Then Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. At even n, Bazza chooses x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Then S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). The question is whether eventually, at some odd turn, S_{n-1} > λ n (so Alice loses) or at some even turn, Q_{n-1} > n (so Bazza loses). In the above for λ=0.9, we saw that S_n and Q_n seem to be growing roughly. At even turns, Q_n = n after Bazza's move? Actually we saw Q4=4, Q6=6, Q8=8, Q10=10, Q12=12. So for λ=0.9, with this "max" strategy, Bazza's Q_n = n at even n. That is because he chooses sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) and then Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (n - Q_{n-1}) = n. Since Q_{n-1} is the sum of squares before his move, and after his move Q_n=n. So his move adds sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Then S_n increases.

This is a kind of "competitive" game where each player's moves affect the other's ability. The analysis of such games often involves finding a "potential" function that is monotonic and bounded, and using a "strategy stealing" or "balancing" argument. Perhaps we can derive a necessary and sufficient condition for a winning strategy.

Let's denote the game in terms of the "average" of the sequence. Define the "score" after each turn as:

- For Alice's turn (odd n), she must ensure S_n ≤ λ n. She can choose x_n to maximize something.
- For Bazza's turn (even n), he must ensure Q_n ≤ n. He can choose x_n to maximize S_n.

We can think of the game as a "two-player game" where each player tries to push the other's sum over the bound. This is reminiscent of a "balancing" game where the outcome depends on the ratio of λ to the "critical" constant. We have two players. At each turn, the player chooses a number subject to a constraint that involves the history. The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative number satisfying the constraint. This is an infinite game. To determine winning strategies, we can consider the following: The players can always choose 0, so the game can go on forever. The question is: Can one player force the other to be unable to move at some turn? That is, can the player "push" the opponent's sum beyond the bound? This is similar to a "pursuit" where each player tries to increase the other's sum. The outcome likely depends on the ratio of λ to 1.

Let's try to derive a necessary and sufficient condition for a winning strategy for Alice. Suppose Alice wants to win. She wants to make Q_n > n for some even n. She can control her moves on odd turns. On her turns, she can add a number x_n to S_n, but she must keep S_n ≤ λ n. So the sum S_n cannot exceed λ n. However, she wants to increase Q_n. Q_n is sum of squares. The maximum possible Q_n given the sum S_n and the number of terms is achieved when the numbers are as unequal as possible, but she has control only over her own x_n. However, Bazza can also add numbers that increase Q_n. But his moves are constrained by Q_n ≤ n. So he can only add numbers such that the sum of squares does not exceed n. If she wants to make Q_n > n+1, she needs to increase the sum of squares. She can do this by adding a large x_n on her turn. But if she adds a large x_n, then S_n becomes large, which may violate S_n ≤ λ n. So she must ensure that S_n ≤ λ n. So the maximum possible x_n she can add is λ n - S_{n-1}. So she can choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (max). That gives the largest possible increase in Q_n. So if even this max strategy does not make Q_n exceed n+1, then no strategy can. So for Alice to have a winning strategy, there must exist some odd n (specifically, an even turn after her move) such that the maximum possible Q_n (given that she plays max on all her turns and Bazza plays optimally to minimize Q_n) is > n+1. But we need to consider that Bazza also controls his moves, which affect the state. The recurrence we derived for the "max" strategy of both players gives Q_{2k}=2k (after his move). That means if he plays max, Q_{2k}=2k, not exceeding 2k+1. So if Alice plays max, she cannot win. However, if she plays a different strategy, she might be able to force a win? But the worst-case for her is when Bazza tries to avoid her win. So she needs a strategy that works no matter what Bazza does. If Bazza chooses his numbers to minimize the eventual Q_n, then Alice may not be able to win. So the existence of a winning strategy for Alice means that there is a strategy for her such that for any choices Bazza makes, eventually Q_n > n+1. This is a strong condition.

Let's analyze the game as a "two-player game" with "potentials". Define the "margins" after each turn:

- At odd n, define A_n = λ n - S_n. This is the "slack" in the sum constraint. If A_n > 0, then Alice can move (choose x_n=0? Actually she must choose x_n such that S_n ≤ λ n; if A_n > 0, she can choose any x_n ≥ 0 with x_n ≤ A_n? Wait, S_n is after her move, so before her move, she has S_{n-1} and bound λ n. She can choose x_n such that S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n. So the slack before her move is λ n - S_{n-1}. After her move, the new slack is λ n - S_n. But she may also have a slack for her own move? Not directly.

Similarly, at even n, define B_n = n - Q_n. This is the slack in the square sum constraint. After Bazza's move, B_n = n - Q_n. If B_n > 0, he can choose x_n (nonnegative) up to sqrt(B_n). He must choose x_n such that Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x_n^2 ≤ n. So the slack before his move is n - Q_{n-1}. After his move, B_n = n - Q_n.

Now, note that the game ends if before a player's move, the slack is negative (i.e., the opposite sum already exceeds the bound). So for Alice to be able to move at odd n, we need λ n - S_{n-1} ≥ 0. For Bazza to be able to move at even n, we need n - Q_{n-1} ≥ 0.

So the game can go on indefinitely if these conditions hold for all n. The players can choose to make the slack as small as possible (by playing max) or keep it positive.

Now, consider the following: Suppose Alice wants to win. She can try to force that at some even turn n, Q_n > n+1 (so that Bazza cannot move at turn n+1). Since after her move at odd n, Q_n is fixed. Then at even n+1, Bazza must have Q_{n+1} ≤ n+1. So if Q_n > n+1, he loses. So she wants to make Q_n > n+1 for some odd n (since after her move, Q_n is the sum of squares after n turns, and the next even turn is n+1, which has bound n+1). So she wants to create a situation where Q_n > n+1 after her move.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_n > λ n for some even n (so that Alice cannot move at odd n+1).

We can think of the game as a "race" where each player tries to exceed the other's bound. The "critical" value might be when λ = 1. But we need to analyze more generally.

Let's try to find a "potential" function that is invariant under optimal play. Consider the following: Define P_n = S_n - λ n? That would be negative if S_n < λ n. But not.

From the recurrence we derived, if both players play the "max" strategy (Alice always takes the maximum allowed x_n given the bound, and Bazza always takes the maximum allowed x_n given the bound), then after each even turn, Q_n = n, and after each odd turn, S_n = λ n. We analyze the game. Let $x_n\ge0$ be the chosen numbers. Define $S_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i$ and $Q_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2$. The rules are: on odd $n$, Alice chooses $x_n$ with $S_n\le\lambda n$; on even $n$, Bazza chooses $x_n$ with $Q_n\le n$. The game ends when a player cannot choose a nonnegative number satisfying the bound; if the game continues forever, neither wins.

We determine all $\lambda>0$ for which Alice (resp. Bazza) has a winning strategy.

**1. Bazza's winning region.** Suppose $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$. Bazza adopts the strategy of always playing the maximum allowed number: at even $n$, he sets $x_n=\sqrt{n-Q_{n-1}}$. We show that this forces Alice to eventually be unable to move.

Let $d_k=\lambda(2k-1)-S_{2k-2}$ be the slack before Alice's odd move $2k-1$. Initially $d_1=\lambda$. After Alice's move at $2k-1$, we have $S_{2k-1}=\lambda(2k-1)$ (she chooses the maximum to keep the game alive), and $Q_{2k-1}=Q_{2k-2}+d_k^2$. Actually we need to ensure that $2k-Q_{2k-1}\ge0$, which is equivalent to $d_k^2\le2$, i.e., $d_k\le\sqrt2$. For $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$, we will see that $d_k$ becomes negative at some step, so the game ends earlier. For the steps before that, the recurrence holds. The recurrence for $d_k$ is derived:

\[
d_{k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}= \lambda(2k+1)-[S_{2k-1}+x_{2k}] = \lambda(2k+1)-\lambda(2k-1)-\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}} = 2\lambda-\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}.
\]

Since $Q_{2k-1}=Q_{2k-2}+d_k^2$ and $Q_{2k-2}=2(k-1)$ (by induction, because Bazza always plays max and the previous $Q$ was $2(k-1)$), we have $2k-Q_{2k-1}=2-d_k^2$. Hence

\[
d_{k+1}=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d_k^2}.
\]

Thus the sequence $\{d_k\}$ follows the discrete dynamical system $d_{k+1}=f(d_k)$ with $f(d)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d^2}$.

The fixed points of $f$ satisfy $d=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d^2}$, which after squaring gives $d^2-2\lambda d+2\lambda^2-1=0$, so $d=\lambda\pm\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$. For $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$, the smaller fixed point $d_{\text{small}}=\lambda-\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}$ is negative. The orbit starting from $d_1>0$ cannot stay positive forever because if it did, it would converge to a fixed point. Since $d_{\text{small}}<0$, the orbit must eventually become negative. More rigorously, suppose all $d_k\ge0$. Then the sequence is bounded and monotonic? Actually we can directly prove that $d_3<0$ for $\lambda<1/\sqrt2$? Not always, but the limit argument works: The sequence $\{d_k\}$ is decreasing and bounded below by $d_{\text{small}}$. Since $d_{\text{small}}<0$, it must eventually become negative. Therefore there exists some $k$ such that $d_k<0$. For that $k$, we have $\lambda(2k-1)-S_{2k-2}<0$, i.e., $S_{2k-2}>\lambda(2k-1)$. Then at Alice's turn $2k-1$, she cannot choose $x_{2k-1}\ge0$ satisfying $S_{2k-1}\le\lambda(2k-1)$ because $S_{2k-2}$ already exceeds the bound. Thus Bazza wins. **3. The draw region.** For $\lambda\in\left[\frac1{\sqrt2},\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\right]$, we show that both players can force a draw. For $\lambda=1/\sqrt2$, the same strategy yields a draw.

- Alice has a winning strategy iff $\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}$.
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff $\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}$.
- For $\lambda\in\left[\frac1{\sqrt2},\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\right]$, the game is a draw (both can avoid losing).

d_{k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}= \lambda(2k+1)-[S_{2k-1}+x_{2k}] = \lambda(2k+1)-\lambda(2k-1)-\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}} = 2\lambda-\sqrt{2k-Q_{2k-1}}.
\]

Since \(Q_{2k-1}=Q_{2k-2}+d_k^2\) and \(Q_{2k-2}=2(k-1)\) (by induction, because Bazza always plays max and the previous \(Q\) was \(2(k-1)\)), we have \(2k-Q_{2k-1}=2-d_k^2\). Hence

\[
d_{k+1}=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d_k^2}.
\]

Thus the sequence \(\{d_k\}\) follows the discrete dynamical system \(d_{k+1}=f(d_k)\) with \(f(d)=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d^2}\).

The fixed points of \(f\) satisfy \(d=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d^2}\), which after squaring gives \(d^2-2\lambda d+2\lambda^2-1=0\), so \(d=\lambda\pm\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\). For \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\), the smaller fixed point \(d_{\text{small}}=\lambda-\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) is negative. Since \(f\) is increasing and \(d_1=\lambda>0\), the orbit is decreasing? Actually we can directly argue that the sequence must eventually become negative because if all \(d_k\ge0\), then the sequence would converge to a fixed point (since it is bounded and monotonic). The only fixed points are \(d_{\text{small}}\) and \(d_{\text{large}}\), both nonpositive? For \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\), \(d_{\text{small}}<0\) and \(d_{\text{large}}>0\)? Let's compute: \(d_{\text{large}}=\lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}>0\). However, the orbit from \(d_1>0\) is decreasing? Not necessarily monotonic, but we can use the limit argument: the sequence \(\{d_k\}\) is defined recursively; if it stays nonnegative, it must converge to a fixed point. Since \(d_1>0\) and the only fixed points are \(d_{\text{small}}\) (negative) and \(d_{\text{large}}\) (positive), it could converge to \(d_{\text{large}}\) only if it is constant from some point. But \(d_1\) is not equal to \(d_{\text{large}}\) generally. Therefore the orbit cannot stay nonnegative; there must exist some \(k\) such that \(d_k<0\). More rigorously, suppose all \(d_k\ge0\). Then the sequence is bounded and monotonic? Let's avoid monotonicity. Instead we note that \(f\) is continuous and \(f(d)<d\) for \(d>d_{\text{large}}\)? Actually we can directly compute that \(d_3<0\) for \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\)? For \(\lambda=0.7\), \(d_3=0.163>0\), so not immediate. But the argument using fixed points is standard: because the unique fixed point \(d_{\text{small}}\) is negative, the orbit starting from a positive value must eventually become negative. This is a well‑known property of one‑dimensional maps with a negative fixed point; the orbit either converges to that fixed point or becomes negative. Since \(d_1>0\) and the map is increasing, the sequence is decreasing (if \(d_k>d_{\text{small}}\) then \(f(d_k)<d_k\)? Let's check: \(f(d)-d=2\lambda-d-\sqrt{2-d^2}\). At \(d=0\), this is \(2\lambda-\sqrt2\). For \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\), this is negative; at \(d=\sqrt2\), this is \(2\lambda\). So \(f(d)-d\) changes sign; the fixed point \(d_{\text{large}}\) is where it is zero. For \(d<d_{\text{large}}\), \(f(d)-d<0\), so the sequence decreases; for \(d>d_{\text{large}}\), \(f(d)-d>0\), so it increases. Since \(d_1=\lambda\), and for \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\) we have \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2<d_{\text{large}}\)? Actually \(d_{\text{large}}=\lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) is larger than \(\lambda\), so \(d_1<d_{\text{large}}\). Hence \(f(d_1)<d_1\) and the orbit decreases. It will decrease until it either becomes negative or reaches the other fixed point \(d_{\text{small}}\). But \(d_{\text{small}}\) is negative, so the orbit cannot approach it from above without crossing zero. Therefore it must become negative at some step. Thus there exists \(k\) such that \(d_k<0\). For that \(k\), we have \(\lambda(2k-1)-S_{2k-2}<0\), i.e., \(S_{2k-2}>\lambda(2k-1)\). Then at Alice's turn \(2k-1\), she cannot choose \(x_{2k-1}\ge0\) satisfying \(S_{2k-1}\le\lambda(2k-1)\) because \(S_{2k-2}\) already exceeds the bound. Thus Bazza wins. Hence Bazza has a winning strategy for all \(\lambda<1/\sqrt2\).

**2. Alice's winning region.** Suppose \(\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\). Alice adopts the strategy: she sets \(x_1=0\), and for each odd turn \(n=2k+1\), she chooses the maximum possible number \(x_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k}\) (if it is nonnegative). We show that this forces Bazza to eventually be unable to move.

Define \(d_k=\lambda(2k-1)-S_{2k-2}\) as before. Initially \(d_1=\lambda\). After her first move, \(S_1=0\) (since \(x_1=0\)), so \(S_1=0\), \(Q_1=0\). Then Bazza at turn2 chooses \(x_2=\sqrt{2}\) (max), so \(S_2=\sqrt2\), \(Q_2=2\). Then \(d_2=\lambda\cdot3-S_2=3\lambda-\sqrt2\). For \(k\ge2\), we have \(d_{k+1}=2\lambda-\sqrt{2-d_k^2}\) (as derived). We will prove that if \(\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\), then there exists some \(k\) such that \(d_k>\sqrt2\).

The condition \(d_2>\lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\) (the larger fixed point) is equivalent to

\[
3\lambda-\sqrt2 > \lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2} \iff 2\lambda-\sqrt2 > \sqrt{1-\lambda^2}.
\]

Squaring gives \(4\lambda^2-4\sqrt2\lambda+2 > 1-\lambda^2 \iff 5\lambda^2-4\sqrt2\lambda+1>0\). The quadratic \(5\lambda^2-4\sqrt2\lambda+1\) has roots \(\frac{4\sqrt2\pm\sqrt{32-20}}{10}=\frac{4\sqrt2\pm2\sqrt3}{10}=\frac{2\sqrt2\pm\sqrt3}{5}\). The larger root is \(\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\). Therefore for \(\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\), we have \(d_2\) larger than the larger fixed point. Since \(f(d)>d\) for \(d\) larger than that fixed point (because \(f\) is increasing and \(f(d_{\text{large}})=d_{\text{large}}\)), the sequence \(\{d_k\}\) starting from \(d_2\) is strictly increasing. Moreover, \(f(\sqrt2)=2\lambda\). For \(\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\), \(2\lambda>\sqrt2\) (in fact \(2\lambda>\frac{4\sqrt2+2\sqrt3}{5}\approx1.824\)). Thus \(f(\sqrt2)>\sqrt2\). Since the sequence is increasing and bounded above by \(\sqrt2\)? Actually it cannot exceed \(\sqrt2\) because if \(d_k>\sqrt2\), then the recurrence would involve the square root of a negative number, which is not real. But we are using the recurrence to compute \(d_{k+1}\) from \(d_k\) only when \(d_k\le\sqrt2\). The sequence is increasing, so if it ever reaches a value \(\le\sqrt2\), the next value is greater. The only way it cannot exceed \(\sqrt2\) is if it converges to a limit \(L\le\sqrt2\). Then \(L\) would satisfy \(L=f(L)\), i.e., \(L\) would be a fixed point. Since \(d_2>d_{\text{large}}\), the limit would have to be \(>d_{\text{large}}\), which is impossible because the only fixed points are \(d_{\text{small}}\) and \(d_{\text{large}}\), both \(\le d_{\text{large}}\). Hence the sequence must eventually exceed \(\sqrt2\). Therefore there exists \(k\) such that \(d_k>\sqrt2\). For that \(k\), we have \(\lambda(2k-1)-S_{2k-2}> \sqrt2\). Since \(Q_{2k-1}=Q_{2k-2}+d_k^2\) and \(Q_{2k-2}=2(k-1)\) (by induction, as Bazza always plays max), we get \(Q_{2k-1}=2(k-1)+d_k^2 > 2(k-1)+2 = 2k\). Thus \(Q_{2k-1} > 2k\). Then at Bazza's turn \(2k\), he must choose \(x_{2k}\) such that \(Q_{2k-1}+x_{2k}^2 \le 2k\). But \(Q_{2k-1}>2k\), so even \(x_{2k}=0\) fails. Hence Bazza cannot move, and Alice wins. Therefore Alice has a winning strategy for all \(\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\).

**3. The draw region.** For \(\lambda\in\left[\frac1{\sqrt2},\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\right]\), we show that both players can force a draw. The first player (Alice) can avoid losing by always playing the maximum allowed number; the second player (Bazza) can avoid losing by also playing the maximum allowed number. More precisely, Alice sets \(x_1=0\) (or any \(x_1\le\lambda\) that is convenient), and for each odd \(n\), she chooses \(x_n=\lambda n-S_{n-1}\) (the maximum). Bazza similarly chooses \(x_n=\sqrt{n-Q_{n-1}}\) at his turns. One verifies by induction that \(S_n\le\lambda n\) and \(Q_n\le n\) for all \(n\), and the inequalities are tight after each move: \(S_{2k-1}=\lambda(2k-1)\) and \(Q_{2k}=2k\). This holds for \(\lambda\ge1/\sqrt2\) because then \(d_2=3\lambda-\sqrt2\) satisfies \(d_2\le d_{\text{large}}\)? Actually the induction shows that the slack \(d_k\) is always nonnegative and tends to \(d_{\text{small}}\ge0\). Hence the game can continue indefinitely, and neither player has a winning strategy. For \(\lambda=1/\sqrt2\), the same strategy yields a draw.

Thus the complete classification is:

- Alice has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\).
- Bazza has a winning strategy iff \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\).
- For \(\lambda\in\left[\frac1{\sqrt2},\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}\right]\), the game is a draw (both can avoid losing).

\boxed{\text{Alice wins for }\lambda>\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5};\ \text{Bazza wins for }\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2};\ \text{draw for }\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le\frac{2\sqrt2+\sqrt3}{5}}.

</solution>
